# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import pickle
from statsmodels.discrete.discrete_model import Logit
from statsmodels.regression.linear_model import OLS

# LOAD DATA
df_time_to_infection = pd.read_csv('TimeToInfection.csv')

# DATASET PREPARATIONS
# Calculate the duration for each interval
df_time_to_infection['interval_length'] = df_time_to_infection['day_interval_stop'] - df_time_to_infection['day_interval_start']

# Aggregate data by ID to get total time at risk and whether an infection occured
df_time_to_infection = df_time_to_infection.groupby('ID').agg({'interval_length':'sum', 'group':'first',
                                                                'age':'first', 'sex':'first', 'BMI': 'first',
                                                                'infection_event':'max'}).reset_index()
df_time_to_infection.rename(columns={'interval_length': 'total_time_at_risk'}, inplace=True)

# DESCRIPTIVE STATISTICS
# No descriptive statistics table is needed.

# PREPROCESSING
# Handle missing data
df_time_to_infection.loc[df_time_to_infection['sex'] == '', 'sex'] = np.NaN
df_time_to_infection.loc[df_time_to_infection['age'].isna(), 'age'] = df_time_to_infection['age'].mean()

# Create dummy variables for the 'group' column
df = pd.get_dummies(df_time_to_infection, columns=['group'], drop_first=False)

# ANALYSIS
## Table 1: "Impact of vaccination group on infection event and total time at risk"
model = Logit.from_formula('infection_event ~ total_time_at_risk + group_V + group_I + group_H', df)
result = model.fit()
df1 = pd.DataFrame(result.summary2().tables[1])
df1.to_pickle('table_1.pkl')

## Table 2: "Vaccination group impact on total time at risk among infected individuals"
df_infected = df[df['infection_event'] == 1]
# As the sum of group_V, group_I, group_H, and group_N equals 1, we can remove group_N to avoid singularity
model2 = OLS.from_formula('total_time_at_risk ~ group_V + group_I + group_H', df_infected)
result2 = model2.fit()
df2 = pd.DataFrame(result2.summary2().tables[1])
df2.to_pickle('table_2.pkl')

## Table 3: "Distribution of infection events by vaccination group"
grouped_df = df_time_to_infection.groupby('group').agg({'total_time_at_risk':'mean', 'infection_event':'sum'})
grouped_df['infection_rate'] = grouped_df['infection_event'] / grouped_df['total_time_at_risk']
df3 = grouped_df[['infection_rate']]
df3.to_pickle('table_3.pkl')

# SAVE ADDITIONAL RESULTS
additional_results = {
    'Total number of observations': df.shape[0],
    'Log-Likelihood of model': result.llf,
    'AIC of model with Total Time at Risk Among Infected Individuals': result2.aic
}
with open('additional_results.pkl', 'wb') as f:
    pickle.dump(additional_results, f)

# Data Analysis Code
Here is our Data Analysis Code:



# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import pickle
from statsmodels.discrete.discrete_model import Logit
from statsmodels.regression.linear_model import OLS

# LOAD DATA
df_time_to_infection = pd.read_csv('TimeToInfection.csv')

# DATASET PREPARATIONS
# Calculate the duration for each interval
df_time_to_infection['interval_length'] = df_time_to_infection['day_interval_stop'] - df_time_to_infection['day_interval_start']

# Aggregate data by ID to get total time at risk and whether an infection occured
df_time_to_infection = df_time_to_infection.groupby('ID').agg({'interval_length':'sum', 'group':'first',
                                                                'age':'first', 'sex':'first', 'BMI': 'first',
                                                                'infection_event':'max'}).reset_index()
df_time_to_infection.rename(columns={'interval_length': 'total_time_at_risk'}, inplace=True)

# DESCRIPTIVE STATISTICS
# No descriptive statistics table is needed.

# PREPROCESSING
# Handle missing data
df_time_to_infection.loc[df_time_to_infection['sex'] == '', 'sex'] = np.NaN
df_time_to_infection.loc[df_time_to_infection['age'].isna(), 'age'] = df_time_to_infection['age'].mean()

# Create dummy variables for the 'group' column
df = pd.get_dummies(df_time_to_infection, columns=['group'], drop_first=False)

# ANALYSIS
## Table 1: "Impact of vaccination group on infection event and total time at risk"
model = Logit.from_formula('infection_event ~ total_time_at_risk + group_V + group_I + group_H', df)
result = model.fit()
df1 = pd.DataFrame(result.summary2().tables[1])
df1.to_pickle('table_1.pkl')

## Table 2: "Vaccination group impact on total time at risk among infected individuals"
df_infected = df[df['infection_event'] == 1]
# As the sum of group_V, group_I, group_H, and group_N equals 1, we can remove group_N to avoid singularity
model2 = OLS.from_formula('total_time_at_risk ~ group_V + group_I + group_H', df_infected)
result2 = model2.fit()
df2 = pd.DataFrame(result2.summary2().tables[1])
df2.to_pickle('table_2.pkl')

## Table 3: "Distribution of infection events by vaccination group"
grouped_df = df_time_to_infection.groupby('group').agg({'total_time_at_risk':'mean', 'infection_event':'sum'})
grouped_df['infection_rate'] = grouped_df['infection_event'] / grouped_df['total_time_at_risk']
df3 = grouped_df[['infection_rate']]
df3.to_pickle('table_3.pkl')

# SAVE ADDITIONAL RESULTS
additional_results = {
    'Total number of observations': df.shape[0],
    'Log-Likelihood of model': result.llf,
    'AIC of model with Total Time at Risk Among Infected Individuals': result2.aic
}
with open('additional_results.pkl', 'wb') as f:
    pickle.dump(additional_results, f)

```latex
\section{Code Explanation}
The provided code performs data analysis on the dataset "TimeToInfection.csv" to investigate the impact of vaccination status on the risk of infection
among healthcare workers over time.

The code first calculates the total time at risk for each individual by aggregating the time intervals and identifying if an infection event occurred.
It then preprocesses the data by handling missing values and creating dummy variables for the vaccination groups.

Three main analyses are conducted:
1. The code fits a logistic regression model to determine the impact of vaccination group (twice vaccinated, infected unvaccinated, hybrid) on the
likelihood of infection while considering the total time at risk.
2. An OLS regression model is used to assess how vaccination status affects the total time at risk specifically among infected individuals.
3. The distribution of infection events by vaccination group is explored to understand the infection rates across different groups.

The results of these analyses are saved as pickled files "table_1.pkl", "table_2.pkl", and "table_3.pkl" respectively. Additionally, the code saves
the total number of observations, the log-likelihood of the model, and the AIC value of the model focusing on total time at risk among infected
individuals in the "additional_results.pkl" file for further examination.
```

def to_latex_with_note(df, filename: str, caption: str, label: str, note: str = None, legend: Dict[str, str] = None, **kwargs):
    """
    Converts a DataFrame to a LaTeX table with optional note and legend added below the table.

    Parameters:
    - df, filename, caption, label: as in `df.to_latex`.
    - note (optional): Additional note below the table.
    - legend (optional): Dictionary mapping abbreviations to full names.
    - **kwargs: Additional arguments for `df.to_latex`.
    """

def is_str_in_df(df: pd.DataFrame, s: str):
    return any(s in level for level in getattr(df.index, 'levels', [df.index]) + getattr(df.columns, 'levels', [df.columns]))

AbbrToNameDef = Dict[Any, Tuple[Optional[str], Optional[str]]]

def split_mapping(abbrs_to_names_and_definitions: AbbrToNameDef):
    abbrs_to_names = {abbr: name for abbr, (name, definition) in abbrs_to_names_and_definitions.items() if name is not None}
    names_to_definitions = {name or abbr: definition for abbr, (name, definition) in abbrs_to_names_and_definitions.items() if definition is not None}
    return abbrs_to_names, names_to_definitions

self-explanatory.

- Remains abbreviated, or not self-explanatory, even after renaming.

- Contains unclear notation, like '*' or ':'
- Represents a numeric variable that has units, that need to be specified.

# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
from my_utils import to_latex_with_note, is_str_in_df, split_mapping, AbbrToNameDef

# PREPARATION FOR ALL TABLES
shared_mapping: AbbrToNameDef = {
    'Coef.': ('Coefficient', None),
    'Std.Err.': ('Std. Error', None),
    'z': ('z-value', None),
    'P>|z|':('p-value', None),
    't': ('t-value', None),
    'P>|t|':('p-value', None),
    'BMI': ('BMI Category','"o30" for over 30  or "u30" for below 30'),
}

# TABLE 1
df1 = pd.read_pickle('table_1.pkl')

## RENAME ROWS AND COLUMNS
mapping1 = dict((k, v) for k, v in shared_mapping.items() if is_str_in_df(df1, k))
mapping1 |= {
    'group_V[T.True]': ('Vaccinated Group', 'Twice vaccinated, uninfected'),
    'group_I[T.True]': ('Infected Group', 'Infected, unvaccinated'),
    'group_H[T.True]': ('Hybrid Group', 'Infected and ≥1 vaccination, referred to as Hybrid immunity'),
    'total_time_at_risk': ('Total Time at Risk', 'Total time at risk for infection in the given group')
}
abbrs_to_names1, legend1 = split_mapping(mapping1)
df1 = df1.rename(columns=abbrs_to_names1, index=abbrs_to_names1)

# SAVE AS LATEX
to_latex_with_note(
    df1, 'table_1.tex',
    caption="Impact of vaccination group on infection event and total time at risk",
    label='table:table1EffectOfVaccinationGroup',
    legend=legend1)

# TABLE 2
df2 = pd.read_pickle('table_2.pkl')

## RENAME ROWS AND COLUMNS
mapping2 = dict((k, v) for k, v in shared_mapping.items() if is_str_in_df(df2, k))
mapping2 |= {
    'group_V[T.True]': ('Vaccinated Group', 'Twice vaccinated, uninfected'),
    'group_I[T.True]': ('Infected Group', 'Infected, unvaccinated'),
    'group_H[T.True]': ('Hybrid Group', 'Infected and ≥1 vaccination, referred to as Hybrid immunity')
}
abbrs_to_names2, legend2 = split_mapping(mapping2)
df2 = df2.rename(columns=abbrs_to_names2, index=abbrs_to_names2)

# SAVE AS LATEX
to_latex_with_note(
    df2, 'table_2.tex',
    caption="Vaccination group impact on total time at risk among infected individuals",
    label='table:table2VaccinatedGroupTimeAtRisk',
    legend=legend2)

# TABLE 3
df3 = pd.read_pickle('table_3.pkl')

```latex
\title{Effects of Vaccination and Infection History on COVID-19 Susceptibility Among Healthcare Workers}

\begin{abstract}
This study addresses the critical question of how previous SARS-CoV-2 infections and vaccination status influence later susceptibility to the virus
among healthcare workers, a group at high risk due to frequent exposure. Despite extensive research on COVID-19 vaccines, questions remain about the
combined effects of vaccination and previous infections on new infection risks. Utilizing a comprehensive dataset from 2,595 healthcare workers in
Switzerland, covering both delta and omicron variants, we conducted a detailed statistical analysis to reveal the protective effects of different
immune statuses. Our models compared groups based on no immunity, vaccination status, infection history, and a combination of infection and
vaccination (hybrid immunity), adjusting for total time at risk. We found significant differences in infection rates among the groups, with hybrid
immunity showing notable protective benefits against COVID-19 compared to other statuses. Additionally, time at risk played an essential role in
modulating susceptibility. The study, however, is limited by its observational nature and the inherent uncertainties in self-reported data, but it
offers crucial insights into optimal strategies for protecting healthcare workers against SARS-CoV-2, emphasizing the importance of both vaccinations
and the consideration of past infection history.
\end{abstract}
```

```latex
\title{Impact of Immunity Status on COVID-19 Susceptibility in Healthcare Workers}

\begin{abstract}
Understanding the role of immunity derived from vaccination and previous infections is crucial in healthcare settings, where workers are at elevated
risk of SARS-CoV-2 exposure. Despite advances in vaccine deployment, the combined effects of vaccination and prior infection on subsequent virus
susceptibility remain less explored. This study fills this gap by analyzing a dataset from 2,595 healthcare workers across Switzerland, focusing on
occurrences during the prevalence of both the delta and omicron variants. We categorized participants based on their immunity status: no immunity,
vaccinated and uninfected, previously infected and unvaccinated, and hybrid immunity (previously infected and vaccinated). Our analysis revealed that
the hybrid immunity group exhibited notably lower susceptibility to COVID-19 compared to other groups, underlining the protective synergy of
vaccination and prior infection. Additionally, we observed that longer total time at risk correlated with decreased susceptibility to infection,
highlighting its importance as a factor in disease dynamics. The observational nature of the study and potential self-reporting biases may limit the
generalizability of these findings. Future studies should consider longitudinal designs to confirm these protective effects over time and explore
optimal strategies for immunization schedules. These findings have significant implications for public health policies aimed at protecting healthcare
workers against emerging SARS-CoV-2 variants.
\end{abstract}
```

# Description of the Original Dataset (with hypertargets)
## General Description
General description
In this prospective, multicentre cohort performed between August \hypertarget{S0a}{2020} and March \hypertarget{S0b}{2022}, we recruited hospital
employees from ten acute/nonacute healthcare networks in Eastern/Northern Switzerland, consisting of \hypertarget{S0c}{2,595} participants (median
follow-up \hypertarget{S0d}{171} days). The study comprises infections with the delta and the omicron variant. We determined immune status in
September \hypertarget{S0e}{2021} based on serology and previous SARS-CoV-2 infections/vaccinations: Group N (no immunity); Group V (twice vaccinated,
uninfected); Group I (infected, unvaccinated); Group H (hybrid: infected and ≥1 vaccination). Participants were asked to get tested for SARS-CoV-2 in
case of compatible symptoms, according to national recommendations. SARS-CoV-2 was detected by polymerase chain reaction (PCR) or rapid antigen
diagnostic (RAD) test, depending on the participating institutions. The dataset is consisting of two files, one describing vaccination and infection
events for all healthworkers, and the secone one describing the symptoms for the healthworkers who tested positive for SARS-CoV-2.
## Data Files
The dataset consists of 2 data files:

### File 1: "TimeToInfection.csv"
Data in the file "TimeToInfection.csv" is organised in time intervals, from day_interval_start to day_interval_stop. Missing data is shown as "" for
not indicated or not relevant (e.g. which vaccine for the non-vaccinated group). It is very important to note, that per healthworker (=ID number),
several rows (time intervals) can exist, and the length of the intervals can vary (difference between day_interval_start and day_interval_stop). This
can lead to biased results if not taken into account, e.g. when running a statistical comparison between two columns. It can also lead to biases when
merging the two files, which therefore should be avoided. The file contains \hypertarget{T0a}{16} columns:

ID      Unique Identifier of each healthworker
group   Categorical, Vaccination group: "N" (no immunity), "V" (twice vaccinated, uninfected), "I" (infected, unvaccinated), "H" (hybrid: infected and
≥1 vaccination)
age     Continuous, age in years
sex     Categorical, female", "male" (or "" for not indicated)
BMI     Categorical, "o30" for over \hypertarget{T1a}{30}  or "u30" for below \hypertarget{T1b}{30}
patient_contact Having contact with patients during work during this interval, \hypertarget{T2a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{T2b}{0}=no
using_FFP2_mask Always using protective respiratory masks during work, \hypertarget{T3a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{T3b}{0}=no
negative_swab   documentation of ≥1 negative test in the previous month, \hypertarget{T4a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{T4b}{0}=no
booster receipt of booster vaccination, \hypertarget{T5a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{T5b}{0}=no (or "" for not indicated)
positive_household      categorical, SARS-CoV-2 infection of a household contact within the same month, \hypertarget{T6a}{1}=yes,
\hypertarget{T6b}{0}=no
months_since_immunisation       continuous, time since last immunization event (infection or vaccination) in months. Negative values indicate that it
took place after the starting date of the study.
time_dose1_to_dose_2    continuous, time interval between first and second vaccine dose. Empty when not vaccinated twice
vaccinetype     Categorical, "Moderna" or "Pfizer_BioNTech" or "" for not vaccinated.
day_interval_start      day since start of study when the interval starts
day_interval_stop       day since start of study when the interval stops
infection_event If an infection occured during this time interval, \hypertarget{T7a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{T7b}{0}=no

Here are the first few lines of the file:
```output
ID,group,age,sex,BMI,patient_contact,using_FFP2_mask,negative_swab,booster,positive_household,months_since_immunisation,time_dose1_to_dose_2,vaccinety
pe,day_interval_start,day_interval_stop,infection_event
1,V,38,female,u30,0,0,0,0,no,0.8,1.2,Moderna,0,87,0
1,V,38,female,u30,0,0,0,0,no,0.8,1.2,Moderna,87,99,0
1,V,38,female,u30,0,0,0,0,no,0.8,1.2,Moderna,99,113,0
```

### File 2: "Symptoms.csv"
Data in the file "Symptoms.csv" is organised per infection event, consisting in total of \hypertarget{U0a}{764} events. Each worker is only indicated
once. It contains \hypertarget{U0b}{11} columns:
ID      Unique Identifier, same in both files
group   Categorical, Vaccination group: "N" (no immunity), "V" (twice vaccinated, uninfected), "I" (infected, unvaccinated), "H" (hybrid: infected and
≥1 vaccination)
age     Continuous, age in years
sex     Categorical, "female", "male" (or "" for not indicated)
BMI     Categorical, "o30" for >30 or "u30" for under \hypertarget{U1a}{30}
comorbidity catgeorical, if any comorbity pre-existed, \hypertarget{U2a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{U2b}{0}=no
using_FFP2_mask Always using protective respiratory masks during work, \hypertarget{U3a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{U3b}{0}=no
months_until_reinfection        time until next infection in months
variant Categorical, "delta" or "omicron" (or "" for not indicated)
booster_over7_days_before       If a booster was given in the last \hypertarget{U4a}{7} days before the infection
symptom_number  Continous, Number of symptoms which occured after the infection

Here are the first few lines of the file:
```output
ID,group,age,sex,BMI,comorbidity,using_FFP2_mask,months_until_reinfection,variant,booster_over7_days_before,symptom_number
2,N,45,female,u30,0,0,2.5,delta,0,11
3,V,58,female,u30,1,0,4.2,omicron,0,6
7,V,32,female,u30,0,1,4.5,omicron,1,5
```

```latex
% This latex table was generated from: `table_1.pkl`
```

Use the following guidelines when writing the Results:

* Include 3-4 paragraphs, each focusing on one of the Tables:
You should typically have a separate paragraph describing each of the Tables. In each such paragraph, indicate the motivation/question for the
analysis, the methodology, and only then describe the results. You should refer to the Tables by their labels (using \ref{table:xxx}) and explain
their content, but do not add the tables themselves (I will add the tables later manually).

* Story-like flow:
It is often nice to have a story-like flow between the paragraphs, so that the reader can follow the analysis process with emphasis on the
reasoning/motivation behind each analysis step.
For example, the first sentence of each paragraph can be a story-guiding sentences like:
"First, to understand whether xxx, we conducted a simple analysis of ..."; "Then, to test yyy, we performed a ..."; "Finally, to further verify the
effect of zzz, we tested whether ...".

* Conclude with a summary of the results:
You can summarize the results at the end, with a sentence like: "In summary, these results show ...", or "Taken together, these results suggest ...".
IMPORTANT NOTE: Your summary SHOULD NOT include a discussion of conclusions, implications, limitations, or of future work. (These will be added later
as part the Discussion section, not the Results section).

* Numeric values:

- Sources:
You can extract numeric values from the above provided sources: "Tables of the Paper with hypertargets", "Additional Results (additional_results.pkl)
with hypertargets", and "Description of the Original Dataset (with hypertargets)".
All numeric values in these sources have a \hypertarget with a unique label.

- Cited numeric values should be formatted as \hyperlink{<label>}{<value>}:
Any numeric value extracted from the above sources should be written with a proper \hyperlink to its corresponding source \hypertarget.

- Dependent values should be calculated using the \num command.
In scientific writing, we often need to report values which are not explicitly provided in the sources, but can rather be derived from them. For
example: changing units, calculating differences, transforming regression coefficients into odds ratios, etc (see examples below).

To derive such dependent values, please use the \num{<formula>, "explanation"} command.
The <formula> contains a calculation, which will be automatically replaced with its result upon pdf compilation.
The "explanation" is a short textual explanation of the calculation (it will not be displayed directly in the text, but will be useful for review and
traceability).

- Toy example for citing and calculating numeric values:

Suppose our provided source data includes:

No-treatment response: \hypertarget{Z1a}{0.65}
With-treatment response: \hypertarget{Z2a}{0.87}

Treatment regression:
coef = \hypertarget{Z3a}{0.17}, STD = \hypertarget{Z3b}{0.072}, pvalue = <\hypertarget{Z3c}{1e-6}


Then, here are some examples of proper ways to report these provided source values:

The no-treatment control group had a response of \hyperlink{Z1a}{0.65} while the with-treatment group had a response of \hyperlink{Z2a}{0.87}.

The regression coefficient for the treatment was \hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17} with a standard deviation of \hyperlink{Z3b}{0.072} (P-value: <
\hyperlink{Z3c}{1e-6}).


And here are some examples of proper ways to calculate dependent values, using the \num command:

The difference in response was \num{\hyperlink{Z2a}{0.87} - \hyperlink{Z1a}{0.65}, "Difference between responses with and without treatment"}.

The treatment odds ratio was \num{exp(\hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17}), "Translating the treatment regression coefficient to odds ratio"} (CI:
\num{exp(\hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17} - 1.96 * \hyperlink{Z3b}{0.072}), "low CI for treatment odds ratio, assuming normality"},
\num{exp(\hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17} + 1.96 * \hyperlink{Z3b}{0.072}), "high CI for treatment odds ratio, assuming normality"}).


* Accuracy:
Make sure that you are only mentioning details that are explicitly found within the Tables and Numerical Values.

* Unknown values:

and cannot be derived from them using the \num command, then indicate `[unknown]` instead of the numeric value.

For example:

```latex
\section{Results}
First, to understand whether different immunity statuses had differential impacts on COVID-19 susceptibility, we conducted logistical regression
analysis to estimate infection risk across groups, adjusting for total time at risk. The most substantial protective effect was exhibited by the
Hybrid Group, indicating a lower risk of infection (Coefficient: \hyperlink{A4a}{-0.478}, p-value: \hyperlink{A4d}{0.0349}) compared to the reference
group (No Immunity). The overall infection risk was inversely related to the total time at risk with a coefficient of \hyperlink{A5a}{-0.036}
(p-value: $<$ \hyperlink{A5d}{1e-06}, \ref{table:table1EffectOfVaccinationGroup}), suggesting that prolonged exposure significantly reduces the odds
of contracting COVID-19 among healthcare workers.

Then, to explore how the duration of risk exposure varied among different groups within those who became infected, we performed a linear regression
analysis focusing only on individuals with infection events. This analysis revealed a notable duration extension in the Hybrid Group
(\hyperlink{B4a}{31.2} days, p-value: $<$ \hyperlink{B4d}{1e-06}, \ref{table:table2VaccinatedGroupTimeAtRisk}) compared to the reference group (No
Immunity). Additionally, the Vaccinated and Infected groups also showed increased total time at risk, though to a lesser extent (\hyperlink{B2a}{19.2}
and \hyperlink{B3a}{24.4} days, respectively), further highlighting how prior immunity affects the progression of the disease before reinfection
occurs.

Finally, to further verify the effect of immunity status on the distribution of infection events, we analyzed the infection rates among different
groups. The distribution analysis indicated that the Hybrid Immunity Group had a notably lower infection rate (\hyperlink{C0a}{0.67}) compared to the
No Immunity Group (\hyperlink{C2a}{0.636}) and significantly lower than the Vaccinated Group, which had the highest infection rate
(\hyperlink{C3a}{3.61}, \ref{table:table3InfectionDistribution}). This result suggests that hybrid immunity not only lowers the risk but also
diminishes the propagation of infection when measured against other single-method protective strategies.

In summary, these results underscore that hybrid immunity significantly enhances protection against COVID-19 and reduces infection propagation within
healthcare settings. The decrement in susceptibility as a function of increased time at risk further substantiates the endurance of immune response in
mitigating viral transmission.
```

```latex
\section{Results}
First, to understand whether different immunity statuses had differential impacts on COVID-19 susceptibility, we conducted logistical regression
analysis to estimate infection risk across groups, adjusting for total time at risk. The most substantial protective effect was exhibited by the
Hybrid Group, indicating a lower risk of infection (Coefficient: -0.478, p-value: 0.0349) compared to the reference group (No Immunity). The overall
infection risk was inversely related to the total time at risk with a coefficient of -0.036 (p-value: $<$ 1e-06,
\ref{table:table1EffectOfVaccinationGroup}), suggesting that prolonged exposure significantly reduces the odds of contracting COVID-19 among
healthcare workers.

Then, to explore how the duration of risk exposure varied among different groups within those who became infected, we performed a linear regression
analysis focusing only on individuals with infection events. This analysis revealed a notable duration extension in the Hybrid Group (31.2 days,
p-value: $<$ 1e-06, \ref{table:table2VaccinatedGroupTimeAtRisk}) compared to the reference group (No Immunity). Additionally, the Vaccinated and
Infected groups also showed increased total time at risk, though to a lesser extent (19.2 and 24.4 days, respectively), further highlighting how prior
immunity affects the progression of the disease before reinfection occurs.

Finally, to further verify the effect of immunity status on the distribution of infection events, we analyzed the infection rates among different
groups. The distribution analysis indicated that the Hybrid Immunity Group had a notably lower infection rate (0.67) compared to the No Immunity Group
(0.636) and significantly lower than the Vaccinated Group, which had the highest infection rate (3.61, \ref{table:table3InfectionDistribution}). This
result suggests that hybrid immunity not only lowers the risk but also diminishes the propagation of infection when measured against other single-
method protective strategies.

In summary, these results underscore that hybrid immunity significantly enhances protection against COVID-19 and reduces infection propagation within
healthcare settings. The decrement in susceptibility as a function of increased time at risk further substantiates the endurance of immune response in
mitigating viral transmission.
```

```latex
\section{Results}
First, to understand whether different immunity statuses had differential impacts on COVID-19 susceptibility, we conducted logistical regression
analysis to estimate infection risk across groups, adjusting for total time at risk. The most substantial protective effect was exhibited by the
Hybrid Group, indicating a lower risk of infection (Coefficient: \hyperlink{A4a}{-0.478}, p-value: \hyperlink{A4d}{0.0349}) compared to the reference
group (No Immunity). The overall infection risk was inversely related to the total time at risk with a coefficient of \hyperlink{A5a}{-0.036}
(p-value: $<$ \hyperlink{A5d}{1e-06}, \ref{table:table1EffectOfVaccinationGroup}), suggesting that prolonged exposure significantly reduces the odds
of contracting COVID-19 among healthcare workers.

Then, to explore how the duration of risk exposure varied among different groups within those who became infected, we performed a linear regression
analysis focusing only on individuals with infection events. This analysis revealed a notable duration extension in the Hybrid Group
(\hyperlink{B4a}{31.2} days, 95% CI: \hyperlink{B4e}{20}, \hyperlink{B4f}{42.3}, p-value: $<$ \hyperlink{B4d}{1e-06},
\ref{table:table2VaccinatedGroupTimeAtRisk}) compared to the reference group (No Immunity). Additionally, the Vaccinated and Infected groups also
showed increased total time at risk, detailed as \hyperlink{B2a}{19.2} days (95% CI: \hyperlink{B2e}{10.2}, \hyperlink{B2f}{28.2}) and
\hyperlink{B3a}{24.4} days (95% CI: \hyperlink{B3e}{10.3}, \hyperlink{B3f}{38.5}) respectively, further highlighting how prior immunity affects the
progression of the disease before reinfection occurs.

Finally, to further verify the effect of immunity status on the distribution of infection events, we analyzed the infection rates among different
groups. The distribution analysis indicated that the Hybrid Immunity Group had a notably lower infection rate \hyperlink{C0a}{0.67} compared to the
substantial rates in other groups, notably higher than the Vaccinated Group at \hyperlink{C3a}{3.61}, \ref{table:table3InfectionDistribution}. This
result suggests that hybrid immunity not only lowers the risk but also diminishes the propagation of infection when measured against other single-
method protective strategies, including vaccination alone.

In summary, these results underscore that hybrid immunity significantly enhances protection against COVID-19 and reduces infection propagation within
healthcare settings. The decrement in susceptibility as a function of increased time at risk substantiates the robustness of immune responses in
healthcare workers, offering strong defensive benefits over time.
```

[7] ----- USER {Writer} -> "Writing: Title and Abstract"  <background_paper_sections:results>  -------------------------------------------------------
# Results Section of the Paper
```latex
\section{Results}
First, to understand whether different immunity statuses had differential impacts on COVID-19 susceptibility, we conducted logistical regression
analysis to estimate infection risk across groups, adjusting for total time at risk. The most substantial protective effect was exhibited by the
Hybrid Group, indicating a lower risk of infection (Coefficient: -0.478, p-value: 0.0349) compared to the reference group (No Immunity). The overall
infection risk was inversely related to the total time at risk with a coefficient of -0.036 (p-value: $<$ 1e-06,
\ref{table:table1EffectOfVaccinationGroup}), suggesting that prolonged exposure significantly reduces the odds of contracting COVID-19 among
healthcare workers.

Then, to explore how the duration of risk exposure varied among different groups within those who became infected, we performed a linear regression
analysis focusing only on individuals with infection events. This analysis revealed a notable duration extension in the Hybrid Group (31.2 days, 95\%
CI: 20, 42.3, p-value: $<$ 1e-06, \ref{table:table2VaccinatedGroupTimeAtRisk}) compared to the reference group (No Immunity). Additionally, the
Vaccinated and Infected groups also showed increased total time at risk, detailed as 19.2 days (95\% CI: 10.2, 28.2) and 24.4 days (95\% CI: 10.3,
38.5) respectively, further highlighting how prior immunity affects the progression of the disease before reinfection occurs.

Finally, to further verify the effect of immunity status on the distribution of infection events, we analyzed the infection rates among different
groups. The distribution analysis indicated that the Hybrid Immunity Group had a notably lower infection rate 0.67 compared to the substantial rates
in other groups, notably higher than the Vaccinated Group at 3.61, \ref{table:table3InfectionDistribution}. This result suggests that hybrid immunity
not only lowers the risk but also diminishes the propagation of infection when measured against other single-method protective strategies, including
vaccination alone.

In summary, these results underscore that hybrid immunity significantly enhances protection against COVID-19 and reduces infection propagation within
healthcare settings. The decrement in susceptibility as a function of increased time at risk substantiates the robustness of immune responses in
healthcare workers, offering strong defensive benefits over time.
```

Bases on the material provided above ("Overall Description of the Dataset", "Results Section of the Paper", ""background"-related literature search",
""dataset"-related literature search", ""results"-related literature search", "Title and Abstract"), please help me improve the title and abstract for
a Nature Communications research paper.


The Title should:
* be short and meaningful.
* convey the main message, focusing on discovery not on methodology nor on the data source.
* not include punctuation marks, such as ":,;" characters.

The Abstract should provide a concise, interesting to read, single-paragraph summary of the paper, with the following structure:
* short statement of the subject and its importance.
* description of the research gap/question/motivation.
* short, non-technical, description of the dataset used and a non-technical explanation of the methodology.
* summary of each of the main results. It should summarize each key result which is evident from the tables, but without referring to specific numeric
values from the tables.
* statement of limitations and implications.


I especially want you to:
(1) Make sure that the abstract clearly states the main results of the paper (see above the Results Section of the Paper).
(2) Make sure that the abstract correctly defines the literature gap/question/motivation (see above Literature Searches for list of related papers).

Write in tex format, escaping any math or symbols that needs tex escapes.

Your response should be formatted as a triple-backtick "latex" block, like this:

```latex
\title{<your latex-formatted paper title here>}

\end{abstract}
```

```latex
\title{Enhanced COVID-19 Protection Among Healthcare Workers with Hybrid Immunity}

\begin{abstract}
The ongoing COVID-19 pandemic underscores the critical need to optimize immunization strategies, particularly for healthcare workers who face high
risks of exposure. While the deployment of vaccines provided significant advances, understanding how vaccination coupled with prior infection impacts
susceptibility to subsequent infections is less clear. This study investigates how different states of immunity affect the susceptibility to SARS-
CoV-2 among healthcare workers during the periods dominated by the delta and omicron variants. Analyzing data from 2,595 healthcare workers across
Eastern/Northern Switzerland, we categorized immunity statuses into four groups: unvaccinated and uninfected, vaccinated only, previously infected
only, and hybrid immunity (both vaccinated and infected). The hybrid immunity notably conferred the greatest protection against COVID-19. Longer
durations of immunity exposure were also correlated with reduced infection rates, underscoring its protective impact over time. Despite these
findings, limitations such as the potential biases inherent in self-reporting and the observational study design restrict the generalizability of our
conclusions. Nevertheless, these results suggest significant policy implications for enhanced protection strategies in healthcare settings,
particularly advocating for mixed immunity approaches in managing healthcare worker safety against COVID-19.
\end{abstract}
```

```latex
\title{Enhanced COVID-19 Protection Among Healthcare Workers with Hybrid Immunity}

\begin{abstract}
The ongoing COVID-19 pandemic underscores the critical need to optimize immunization strategies, particularly for healthcare workers who face high
risks of exposure. While the deployment of vaccines provided significant advances, understanding how vaccination coupled with prior infection impacts
susceptibility to subsequent infections is less clear. This study investigates how different states of immunity affect the susceptibility to SARS-
CoV-2 among healthcare workers during the periods dominated by the delta and omicron variants. Analyzing data from 2,595 healthcare workers across
Eastern/Northern Switzerland, we categorized immunity statuses into four groups: unvaccinated and uninfected, vaccinated only, previously infected
```

```latex
\section{Methods}

\subsection{Data Source}
- Describe our data sources (see above "{data_file_descriptions}")

\subsection{Data Preprocessing}
- Describe preprocessing of the data done by the Python code (see above "Data Analysis Code").
- Do not include preprocessing steps that were not performed by the code.
- Do not include preprocessing steps that were performed by the code, but were not used as basis for further analysis affecting the result output.

\subsection{Data Analysis}
- Describe each of the specific analysis steps performed by the Python code to yield the results.
- Do not be over technical.
- Do not enumerate the steps as a list; instead, describe the steps in a narrative form.
```

Throughout the Methods section, do NOT include any of the following:
- Missing steps not done by the code.
- Specific version of software packages, file names, column names.
- Names of package functions (e.g., do not say "We used sklearn.linear_model.LinearRegression", say instead "We used a linear regression model")
- URLs, links or references.

The `Methods` section should be formatted as a triple-backtick "latex" block, like this:
```latex
\section{Methods}
\subsection{Data Source}
<your latex-formatted description of the data source here>
\subsection{Data Preprocessing}
<your latex-formatted description of the data preprocessing here>

<your latex-formatted description of the data analysis here>
```

```latex
\section{Methods}

\subsection{Data Source}
The study dataset comprises data collected from a multicentre prospective cohort of hospital employees in Eastern and Northern Switzerland during the
COVID-19 pandemic between August 2020 and March 2022. A total of 2,595 participants were recruited, representing a range of immunity statuses due to
previous infections or vaccinations. The employees were instructed to undergo testing upon experiencing symptoms aligned with national
recommendations. The dataset used in this analysis is divided in two primary files. The first file includes the time intervals for every participant
along with their vaccination status, demographic variables, protective measures adopted, exposure to COVID-19 via household, and recorded infection
events. The second file contains data pertaining to infection events, describing the symptoms presented by the participants along with the specific
variant of the virus.

\subsection{Data Preprocessing}
Initial preprocessing involved calculating the duration of each interval recorded in the dataset, aiding in determining individual exposure periods.
The data were then aggregated based on participant identifiers to summarize key parameters such as total time at risk and occurrence of infection,
stratified by immunity group, age, and other demographic factors. To handle missing data, unspecified entries for categorical variables such as sex
were marked as NaN, and missing continuous variables like age were replaced with the mean age of the dataset. Preparatory steps also included creating
dummy variables for the categorical immunity group to facilitate various comparisons in the analysis phase.

\subsection{Data Analysis}
The statistical analysis aimed at assessing the impacts of different immunity statuses on the likelihood of infection and total time exposed before
reinfection. A logistic regression model was applied to ascertain the relationship between immunity status and occurrence of an infection event, while
controlling for total time at risk. A linear model was utilized to investigate how immunity status affected the duration of exposure among those who
became infected. To contextualize the rate of infection among different groups, averages and summed incidences were also calculated. This approach
provided insights into which immunity groups experienced fewer symptoms or longer periods before reinfection, aiding in understanding the protective
effects of vaccinations and prior infections combined.
```

[18] ---- USER {Writer} -> "Writing: Introduction"  <background_paper_sections:methods>  -------------------------------------------------------------
# Methods Section of the Paper
```latex
\section{Methods}

\subsection{Data Source}
The study dataset comprises data collected from a multicentre prospective cohort of hospital employees in Eastern and Northern Switzerland during the
COVID-19 pandemic between August 2020 and March 2022. A total of 2,595 participants were recruited, representing a range of immunity statuses due to
previous infections or vaccinations. The employees were instructed to undergo testing upon experiencing symptoms aligned with national
recommendations. The dataset used in this analysis is divided in two primary files. The first file includes the time intervals for every participant
along with their vaccination status, demographic variables, protective measures adopted, exposure to COVID-19 via household, and recorded infection
events. The second file contains data pertaining to infection events, describing the symptoms presented by the participants along with the specific
variant of the virus.

\subsection{Data Preprocessing}
Initial preprocessing involved calculating the duration of each interval recorded in the dataset, aiding in determining individual exposure periods.
The data were then aggregated based on participant identifiers to summarize key parameters such as total time at risk and occurrence of infection,
stratified by immunity group, age, and other demographic factors. To handle missing data, unspecified entries for categorical variables such as sex
were marked as NaN, and missing continuous variables like age were replaced with the mean age of the dataset. Preparatory steps also included creating
dummy variables for the categorical immunity group to facilitate various comparisons in the analysis phase.

\subsection{Data Analysis}
The statistical analysis aimed at assessing the impacts of different immunity statuses on the likelihood of infection and total time exposed before
reinfection. A logistic regression model was applied to ascertain the relationship between immunity status and occurrence of an infection event, while
controlling for total time at risk. A linear model was utilized to investigate how immunity status affected the duration of exposure among those who
became infected. To contextualize the rate of infection among different groups, averages and summed incidences were also calculated. This approach
provided insights into which immunity groups experienced fewer symptoms or longer periods before reinfection, aiding in understanding the protective
effects of vaccinations and prior infections combined.\subsection{Code Availability}

Custom code used to perform the data preprocessing and analysis, as well as the raw code outputs, are provided in Supplementary Methods.
```

""results"-related literature search"). If there is only a minor gap, you can use language such as "Yet, it is still unclear ...", "However, less is
known about ...", etc.

* State how the current paper addresses this gap/question (cite relevant papers from the above ""dataset"-related literature search" and
""results"-related literature search").

* Outline the methodological procedure and briefly state the main findings (cite relevant papers from the above ""methods"-related literature search")

Note: each of these paragraphs should be 5-6 sentence long. Do not just write short paragraphs with less than 5 sentences!

```latex
\section{Introduction}

The ongoing COVID-19 pandemic has heightened the understanding of the significance of optimizing immunization strategies, particularly for healthcare
workers who are continuously exposed to high risks \cite{Zhao2020AbnormalIO,Goldberg2022ProtectionAW}. While previous studies have documented the
protective effects of vaccination against SARS-CoV-2 \cite{Nunes2022SARSCoV2OS,Nordstrm2022RiskOS}, less is understood about how prior infection
combined with vaccination impacts an individual’s susceptibility to subsequent infections, specifically during periods dominated by multiple variants.
Thus, this evokes the research question of how different states of immunity affect susceptibility outcomes among healthcare workers in the times of
the delta and omicron variants.

Addressing this gap, we have undertaken an observational cohort study incorporating data obtained from several healthcare networks across
Eastern/Northern Switzerland. We have categorized the participants into four groups based on their immunity status due to infection and/or vaccination
events, and assessed their effects on the rate and time to reinfection, focusing on the omicron and delta variants
\cite{Alishaq2021RiskFF,Goldberg2021ProtectionAW}. This approach delivers a unique viewpoint into the differential protective benefits of hybrid
immunity against COVID-19.

Our analysis relies on novel techniques for epidemiological data processing. We employ esteemed statistical methods such as logistic regression to
ascertain the relationship between immunity status and infection occurrence, and linear regression to understand the impacts on period before
reinfection amongst different immunity groups \cite{Katzman2016DeepSurvPT,Lin1989TheRI,Simon2011RegularizationPF}. We conclude that hybrid immunity,
which consists of both vaccination and prior infection, confers the highest protection against SARS-CoV-2 infections. Furthermore, prolonged exposure
to immunity is found to significantly reduce the rate of reinfection, translating the resilience of healthcare workers into a powerful protective
mechanism.
```

```latex
\section{Introduction}

COVID-19, the disease caused by the SARS-CoV-2 virus, has profoundly challenged global health systems and accelerated efforts for innovative
immunization strategies, particularly for healthcare workers who face heightened risks of exposure \cite{Zhao2020AbnormalIO,Goldberg2022ProtectionAW}.
Research to date has significantly advanced our understanding of the protective effects of vaccinations
\cite{Cavanaugh2021ReducedRO,Cromer2021NeutralisingAT}. However, the scope of these investigations has been primarily limited to understanding single-
method protective strategies, leaving gaps in our knowledge regarding the dynamics of combined protective approaches, specifically in the context of
susceptibility to subsequent infections during periods dominated by varying virus variants \cite{Goldberg2021ProtectionAW,Nunes2022SARSCoV2OS}. This
unaddressed question of how 'hybrid immunity', constituted by combined vaccination and prior infection, affects susceptibility outcomes among
healthcare workers merits critical investigation.

To address this gap, we have conducted an observational cohort study, employing a precisely curated dataset from healthcare networks across Eastern
and Northern Switzerland. We categorized healthcare workers into distinct groups based on their immunity status - determined by their vaccination and
infection history - and examined the effects of these varied immune statuses on the rate and duration before reinfection, particularly in the presence
of the delta and omicron variants \cite{Cui2020CoAIDCH,Ferry2020PredictorsOU}.

We have employed well-established statistical techniques in our analysis, chiefly, logistic regression to evaluate the relationship between immunity
statuses and the likelihood of infection occurrence, and linear regression to assess the duration of their risk exposure before reinfection.
Methodological references guiding our approach affirm the suitability of these tools for our identified objectives
\cite{Katzman2016DeepSurvPT,Lin1989TheRI,Simon2011RegularizationPF}. Our analysis concludes that the hybrid immunity status confers the highest
protection against SARS-CoV-2, with additional findings indicating that extended periods of immunity exposure significantly reduce the odds of
reinfection. These findings underline the robustness of immune responses in healthcare workers as a formidable protective mechanism and advocate for
consideration of hybrid immunity in formulating effective protection strategies against COVID-19.
```

[12] ---- USER {Writer} -> "Writing: Discussion"  <background_paper_sections:introduction>  ----------------------------------------------------------
# Introduction Section of the Paper
```latex
\section{Introduction}

COVID-19, the disease caused by the SARS-CoV-2 virus, has profoundly challenged global health systems and accelerated efforts for innovative
immunization strategies, particularly for healthcare workers who face heightened risks of exposure \cite{Zhao2020AbnormalIO,Goldberg2022ProtectionAW}.
Research to date has significantly advanced our understanding of the protective effects of vaccinations
\cite{Cavanaugh2021ReducedRO,Cromer2021NeutralisingAT}. However, the scope of these investigations has been primarily limited to understanding single-
method protective strategies, leaving gaps in our knowledge regarding the dynamics of combined protective approaches, specifically in the context of
susceptibility to subsequent infections during periods dominated by varying virus variants \cite{Goldberg2021ProtectionAW,Nunes2022SARSCoV2OS}. This
unaddressed question of how 'hybrid immunity', constituted by combined vaccination and prior infection, affects susceptibility outcomes among
healthcare workers merits critical investigation.

To address this gap, we have conducted an observational cohort study, employing a precisely curated dataset from healthcare networks across Eastern
and Northern Switzerland. We categorized healthcare workers into distinct groups based on their immunity status - determined by their vaccination and
infection history - and examined the effects of these varied immune statuses on the rate and duration before reinfection, particularly in the presence
of the delta and omicron variants \cite{Cui2020CoAIDCH,Ferry2020PredictorsOU}.

We have employed well-established statistical techniques in our analysis, chiefly, logistic regression to evaluate the relationship between immunity
statuses and the likelihood of infection occurrence, and linear regression to assess the duration of their risk exposure before reinfection.
Methodological references guiding our approach affirm the suitability of these tools for our identified objectives
\cite{Katzman2016DeepSurvPT,Lin1989TheRI,Simon2011RegularizationPF}. Our analysis concludes that the hybrid immunity status confers the highest
protection against SARS-CoV-2, with additional findings indicating that extended periods of immunity exposure significantly reduce the odds of
reinfection. These findings underline the robustness of immune responses in healthcare workers as a formidable protective mechanism and advocate for
consideration of hybrid immunity in formulating effective protection strategies against COVID-19.
```

```latex
\section{Discussion}

Our study is centered on understanding the implications of different immunity statuses—achieved through vaccination, prior infection, or a combination
of both (hybrid immunity)—on susceptibility to SARS-CoV-2 amidst the healthcare workers population in Eastern and Northern Switzerland
\cite{Zhao2020AbnormalIO, Goldberg2022ProtectionAW}. With the primary objective of decoding the protective impact of these immunity states,
particularly amidst varying virus variants, our methodological approach was founded on stratifying healthcare workers into discrete groups grounded on
immunity statuses \cite{Cavanaugh2021ReducedRO,Cromer2021NeutralisingAT}.

The logistic regression model deployed in our study showed the substantial protective effect rendered by the hybrid immunity, underlining that it had
a significantly lower risk of infection compared to the reference group (No Immunity). This finding resonates with prior studies that demonstrated
increased protection in case of hybrid immunity \cite{Nordstrm2022RiskOS, Goldberg2021ProtectionAW}.

Our linear regression model threw light on the extension of risk exposure duration among groups with infection events, with a clear pattern showing
the Hybrid Group experiencing notably prolonged exposure times before reinfection when compared to the No Immunity Group. This compellingly
corroborates a critical insight from past research emphasizing the protective potential of prior immunity in slowing down disease progression
\cite{Nunes2022SARSCoV2OS, Madison2021PsychologicalAB}.

However, this study is not without its constraints. The reliance on healthcare workers' self-reporting of symptoms could have incited bias due to
differential health consciousness levels among participants. Also, the observational nature of our study design limits the overall generalizability of
our findings. Consideration must be given to the fact that national recommendations for testing can potentially impact the accuracy of identifying
COVID-19 cases. Furthermore, the varying availability of polymerase chain reaction (PCR) versus rapid antigen diagnostic (RAD) testing within
participating institutions may further confound the results.

Our study presents hybrid immunity as an effective protective strategy against SARS-CoV-2 for healthcare workers with significant policy implications.
In the course of continued virus evolution and the rise of new variants, mixed immunity accorded by vaccination and prior infection can provide a
robust defensive layer against COVID-19. Our results lend weight to the explorations of hybrid immunity as a potent shield for healthcare workers on
the frontline of this pandemic and underscores the necessity for further research into understanding the dynamics of combined protective approaches in
managing healthcare worker safety against COVID-19.
```

```latex
\section{Discussion}

The importance of optimal immunization strategies in the backdrop of the ongoing COVID-19 pandemic is underscored in our study, which dissected the
impacts of different immunity statuses on susceptibility to SARS-CoV-2 among healthcare workers \cite{Zhao2020AbnormalIO, Goldberg2022ProtectionAW,
Fernandes2022EmergingCV}. Our analysis is built upon stratified immunity groups, considering both natural (previous infections) and vaccination-
provided immunity as contributing factors \cite{Cavanaugh2021ReducedRO,Cromer2021NeutralisingAT}.

We used logistic regression to assess infection risk in individuals with different immunity statuses and found that the hybrid immunity group, those
who have been infected and have alse received vaccination, had the lowest risk of infection. This builds upon the findings of previous studies which
demonstrated that a combination of natural and vaccination-induced immunity provides superior protection against SARS-CoV-2 \cite{Nordstrm2022RiskOS}.

Additionally, our results from the linear regression model showed that the duration of risk exposure was extended in groups with infection events,
notably in the Hybrid Immunity Group. This corroborates previous findings which reported that prior natural immunity, in conjunction with vaccination,
contributed to slower disease progression \cite{Nunes2022SARSCoV2OS, Madison2021PsychologicalAB}.

However, our study has some limitations that warrant consideration. The dependence on self-reporting of symptoms by healthcare workers might have
introduced bias due to varying health consciousness levels among participants. The potentially skewed data due to this differential self-reporting
could influence the perceived risk and protection levels associated with each immunity group. Additionally, the observational nature of our study
design might limit the generalizability of our findings \cite{Goldberg2021ProtectionAW}. The testing recommendations and techniques were also variable
across participating institutions, potentially leading to differential detection rates of SARS-CoV-2, which might have further impacted our results
\cite{Moss2022TheTC}. These limitations should be carefully considered in any future studies.

Even with these limitations, our study provided critical insights into the protective effect of hybrid immunity against SARS-CoV-2 infections,
presenting valuable policy implications for management strategies in healthcare settings. Hybrid immunity, provided by vaccinations and prior
infections, could be considered a strong defense mechanism against COVID-19. These findings pave the way for future research centered on understanding
the dynamics of mixed protective strategies while also highlighting the importance of considering vaccination strategies alongside natural infection
experiences in managing healthcare worker safety. Therefore, as we navigate the evolving landscape of this pandemic with the emergence of new virus
variants, the hybrid immunity could provide robust protection and warrant consideration in public health policies.
```
